### [1] [ReactiveGWM: Steering NPC in Reactive Game World Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15256)
*Zeqing Wang,Danze Chen,Zhaohu Xing,Zizhao Tong,Yinhan Zhang,Xingyi Yang,Yeying Jin*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: ReactiveGWM decouples player control from NPC behavior in a diffusion-based game world model using additive bias for inputs and cross-attention for high-level NPC responses, enabling zero-shot transfer to other games.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current game world models are player-centric and treat NPCs as background visuals, failing to capture action-induced NPC reactivity; a true simulation engine requires physical understanding and responsive NPCs that can adapt to player actions across games.

Method: Injects player actions into a diffusion backbone via lightweight additive bias; uses cross-attention modules to ground NPC responses (Offense, Control, Defense) with a game-agnostic interactive logic representation; enables zero-shot transfer by plugging learned modules into unannotated world models of different games.

Result: Evaluated on two Street Fighter games; preserves fine-grained player controllability while achieving robust, prompt-aligned NPC strategy adherence.

Conclusion: ReactiveGWM enables steerable, strategy-rich NPC interactions without domain-specific retraining, offering scalable interaction with NPCs across different games.

Abstract: Current game world models simulate environments from a subjective, player-centric perspective. However, by treating the Non-Player Character (NPC) merely as background pixels, these models cannot capture interactions between the player and NPC. In that sense, they act as passive video renderers rather than real simulation engines, lacking the physical understanding needed to model action-induced NPC reactivities. We introduce ReactiveGWM, a reactive game world model that synthesizes dynamic interactions between the player and NPC. Instead of entangling all interaction dynamics, ReactiveGWM explicitly decouples player controls from NPC behaviors. Player actions are injected into the diffusion backbone via a lightweight additive bias, while high-level NPC responses (e.g., Offense, Control, Defense) are grounded through cross-attention modules. Crucially, these modules learn a game-agnostic representation of interactive logic. This enables zero-shot strategy transfer: our learned modules can be plugged directly into off-the-shelf, unannotated world models of different games. This instantly unlocks steerable NPC interactions without any domain-specific retraining. Evaluated on two Street Fighter games, ReactiveGWM maintains fine-grain player controllability while achieving robust, prompt-aligned NPC strategy adherence, paving the way for scalable, strategy-rich interaction with the NPC.

</details>


### [2] [Deep Pre-Alignment for VLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15300)
*Tianyu Yu,Kechen Fang,Zihao Wan,Kaidong Zhang,Yicheng Zhang,Jun Song,Bo Zheng,Yuan Yao*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Deep Pre-Alignment (DPA) replaces the ViT encoder with a small VLM perceiver to align visual features with the LLM text space early, improving multimodal performance and reducing language forgetting, with gains across 4B–32B scales and across LLM families.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Visual features remain distant from the text space in initial LLM layers, causing misalignment and wasted depth that hurts deep understanding and reasoning. A deep alignment via a perceiver can bridge the gap.

Method: Replace the standard ViT visual encoder with a compact VLM perceiver (Deep Pre-Alignment). This decouples alignment from the LLM, pushing alignment into a dedicated perceiver module with marginal computation overhead, and allows the VLM to align visual features deeply with the text space prior to LLM processing.

Result: Empirical gains: +1.9 points on 8 multimodal benchmarks at 4B; +3.0 points at 32B. 32.9% reduction in language capability forgetting across 3 text benchmarks. Gains generalize across LLM families such as Qwen3 and LLaMA 3.2.

Conclusion: DPA provides a general, scalable upgrade path for VLMs, enabling deep alignment with minimal overhead and transferability across LLM families, representing a modular update to visual encoders for better multimodal reasoning.

Abstract: Most Vision Language Models (VLMs) directly map outputs from ViT encoders to the LLM via a lightweight projector. While effective, recent analysis suggests this architecture suffers from an alignment challenge: visual features remain distant from the text space in the initial layers of the LLM, forcing the model to waste critical depth~\cite{zhang-etal-2024-investigating,artzy-schwartz-2024-attend} on superficial modality alignment rather than deep understanding and complex reasoning. In this work, we propose Deep Pre-Alignment (DPA), a novel architecture that replaces the standard ViT encoder with a small VLM as perceiver, ensuring visual features are deeply aligned with the text space of the target large language model. Comprehensive experiments demonstrate the effectiveness of DPA. On the 4B parameter scale, DPA outperforms baselines by 1.9 points across 8 multimodal benchmarks, with gains widening to 3.0 points at the 32B scale. Moreover, by offloading alignment to the perceiver, DPA achieves a 32.9\% reduction in language capability forgetting over 3 text benchmarks. We further demonstrate that these gains are consistent across different LLM families including Qwen3 and LLaMA 3.2, highlighting the generality of our approach. Beyond performance, DPA also offers a seamless upgrade path for current VLM development, requiring only a modular replacement for the visual encoder with marginal computation overhead.

</details>


### [3] [One Pass Is Not Enough: Recursive Latent Refinement for Generative Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15309)
*Mehdi Esmaeilzadeh,Alexia Jolicoeur-Martineau,Chirag Vashist,Ke Li*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: RTM introduces iterative refinement in style-based generators to improve both image quality and diversity; when combined with IMLE, it achieves leading precision/recall and competitive FID across CIFAR-10, CelebA-HQ 256, nine few-shot sets, and improves StyleGAN2/ADA on 512x512 datasets, outperforming flow-matching baselines on recall/precision.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: FID saturates and conflates fidelity with mode coverage; precision and recall are necessary complements to capture both quality and diversity; current generative models often optimize fidelity at the expense of coverage, risking mode collapse.

Method: Replace the single-pass latent mapping in style-based generators with an iterative refinement (recursive refinement) process (RTM); integrate with IMLE to explicitly optimize mode coverage; demonstrate generality beyond IMLE by improving existing StyleGAN2/ADA baselines.

Result: RTM achieves the highest precision and recall among current state-of-the-art approaches while maintaining competitive FID; improvements across CIFAR-10, CelebA-HQ@256, and nine few-shot benchmarks; also improves StyleGAN2/ADA on CIFAR-10 and AFHQ-v1@512; the gains are not limited to IMLE, and recursive refinement improves both quality and diversity.

Conclusion: Recursive refinement in style-based generators provides a general, effective route to simultaneously improve fidelity and coverage, addressing FID saturation and reducing mode collapse; the approach yields robust gains across multiple datasets and architectures, and outperforms flow-matching baselines on recall/precision.

Abstract: Despite remarkable progress, image generation is far from solved. The dominant metric, FID, conflates sample fidelity with mode coverage and is close to being saturated. Yet a model can still exhibit mode collapse while achieving a low FID, since a handful of sharp, near-duplicate images can outscore a model that faithfully covers the full data distribution. We argue that precision and recall are essential complements to FID, and that because FID is already saturated, the more meaningful goal is to improve diversity and coverage. Achieving high recall requires a model that explicitly prioritizes mode coverage, unlike most generative models, which optimize sample fidelity. We introduce RTM, which replaces the single-pass latent mapping in style-based generators with an iterative refinement process, and show that this consistently improves both quality and diversity. Integrated with Implicit Maximum Likelihood Estimation (IMLE), which optimizes mode coverage by design, RTM achieves the highest precision and recall among current state-of-the-art approaches while maintaining competitive FID, with improvements across CIFAR-10, CelebA-HQ at 256x256, and nine few-shot benchmarks. RTM also improves StyleGAN2 and StyleGAN2-ADA on CIFAR-10 and AFHQ-v1 at 512x512, demonstrating that the benefit is not specific to IMLE. Unlike flow-matching baselines that achieve competitive FID at the expense of coverage, recursive refinement improves both quality and diversity simultaneously.

</details>


### [4] [COPRA: Conditional Parameter Adaptation with Reinforcement Learning for Video Anomaly Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15325)
*Darryl Cherian Jacob,Xinyu Liu,Kai Wang,Pan He*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: COPRA dynamically adapts a frozen Vision-Language Model (VLM) for video anomaly detection by generating input-specific weight updates for each video segment, addressing training-testing distribution and sampling inconsistencies, yielding strong in-domain and cross-domain performance and transfer to other tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Addresses the fundamental mismatch between training and inference in VAD: (1) reliance on static post-training adaptation limits generalization to distribution shifts; (2) training on sparse frames while inference uses densely sampled segments, causing training-testing inconsistency.

Method: A conditional parameter adaptation framework that, for each video segment, produces input-dependent parameter updates to modulate a frozen VLM. This weight-space generation replaces fixed prompts or shared fine-tuning, enabling dynamic, context-aware adaptation during both training and inference.

Result: Empirical results show strong performance on standard VAD benchmarks, outperforming static baselines in both in-domain and cross-domain settings. COPRA also generalizes to unseen tasks such as multiple-choice Video QA and Dense Captioning.

Conclusion: COPRA constitutes an effective, scalable framework for adaptive video understanding that leverages per-input weight updates to align training and inference, with cross-task generalization and potential future release of code.

Abstract: Vision-language models (VLMs) have shown strong performance in video anomaly detection (VAD) while providing interpretable predictions. However, existing VLM-based VAD methods suffer from a fundamental mismatch between training and inference in both data distribution and model configuration. First, most approaches rely on static post-training adaptation, limiting generalization under distribution shifts such as unseen environments or anomaly types. Second, they train VLMs on sparse frames from long videos, but perform inference on densely sampled short segments, creating inconsistencies between training and testing. To address these limitations, we propose COPRA, a conditional parameter adaptation framework for VLM-based VAD. Instead of fixed prompts or shared parameter updates, COPRA generates input-specific parameter updates to dynamically adapt a frozen VLM for each video segment during both training and inference. Experiments show strong performance on standard VAD benchmarks, consistently outperforming static baselines in both in-domain and cross-domain settings. Moreover, COPRA generalizes beyond VAD to unseen tasks such as multiple-choice Video Question Answering and Dense Captioning. These results highlight COPRA as an effective weight-space generation framework for scalable, adaptive, and context-aware video understanding. The code will be released at https://github.com/THE-MALT-LAB/COPRA

</details>


### [5] [Multimodal Object Detection Under Sparse Forest-Canopy Occlusion](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15326)
*Nitik Jain,Mangal Kothari*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Multimodal sensing approach for forest-mediated human detection combining LiDAR, visible–thermal fusion, and synthetic-aperture imaging; LiDAR penetration is limited, while fusion and AOS improve visibility and ground-plane detection; YOLOv5 mAP ≈ 0.83 on FLIR classes; establishes a UAV-relevant baseline and points to forest-specific datasets and real-time multimodal integration.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Forest environments pose strong, structured occlusion and clutter that hinder reliable human detection. The work seeks a practical, multimodal pipeline to enable UAV-based search-and-rescue and surveillance by assessing active sensing feasibility, enhancing saliency in thermal imagery, and suppressing canopy clutter.

Method: Three components: (i) experimental evaluation of LiDAR returns through vegetation to assess active sensing feasibility; (ii) visible–thermal image fusion using a multi-scale transform and sparse-representation framework to boost human saliency; (iii) synthetic-aperture imaging via Airborne Optical Sectioning (AOS) to suppress canopy clutter. A YOLOv5 detector is fine-tuned on the Teledyne FLIR thermal dataset and evaluated on thermal and fused images.

Result: Terrestrial LiDAR configuration provides limited object-level penetration; visible–thermal fusion improves target visibility in low-contrast scenes; AOS enhances ground-plane detection in synthetic forest imagery. The fine-tuned YOLOv5 achieves mean average precision ≈ 0.83 on the top three FLIR classes.

Conclusion: The study yields an initial baseline for UAV-deployable search-and-rescue and surveillance in forested environments and highlights the need for dedicated forest datasets and real-time multimodal integration for robust operation.

Abstract: Reliable detection of humans beneath forest canopy remains a difficult remote-sensing challenge due to sparse, structured, and viewpoint-dependent occlusion. This paper presents a multimodal proof-of-concept pipeline that integrates three complementary approaches: (i) experimental evaluation of LiDAR returns through vegetation to assess the feasibility of active sensing, (ii) visible--thermal image fusion using a multi-scale transform and sparse-representation framework to enhance human saliency, and (iii) synthetic-aperture image formation via Airborne Optical Sectioning (AOS) to suppress canopy clutter. A YOLOv5 detector is fine-tuned on the Teledyne FLIR thermal dataset and evaluated on thermal and fused imagery. Results show that the tested terrestrial LiDAR configuration provides limited penetration for object-level detection, while visible--thermal fusion improves target visibility in low-contrast scenes and AOS enhances ground-plane detection in synthetic forest imagery. The fine-tuned YOLOv5 achieves a mean average precision of $\sim$0.83 on the top three FLIR classes. These findings establish an initial baseline for UAV-deployable search-and-rescue and surveillance systems operating in forested environments, and motivate future work on dedicated forest datasets and real-time multimodal integration.

</details>


### [6] [Minerva-Ego: Spatiotemporal Hints for Egocentric Video Understanding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15342)
*Arsha Nagrani,Jasper Uijilings,Shyamal Buch,Tobias Weyand,Sudheendra Vijayanarasimhan,Bo Hu,Ramin Mehran,David A Ross,Cordelia Schmid*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Introduces Minerva-Ego, a new egocentric video reasoning benchmark with multi-step multimodal questions and spatiotemporal reasoning traces; shows a substantial gap between state-of-the-art models and humans; prompts providing 'where'/'when' cues improve performance; dataset includes spatiotemporal object masks and is publicly available.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To enable evaluation of intermediate, multi-step reasoning in egocentric video understanding and multimodal reasoning, beyond final textual answers, by providing traces and precise object localization.

Method: Augment existing egocentric video datasets with challenging multi-step multimodal questions and spatiotemporal reasoning traces; annotate objects of interest as spatiotemporal masks; perform benchmarking to analyze model performance and prompting strategies, including hints about 'where' and 'when' to look.

Result: Significant performance gap remains between state-of-the-art models and human performance; prompting frontier models with hints about 'where' and 'when' to look yields notable accuracy gains; dataset provides richer evaluation, including object-level masks; code/dataset released at the provided GitHub link.

Conclusion: Minerva-Ego enables richer evaluation of egocentric video reasoning and demonstrates that targeted prompting cues can substantially improve performance, highlighting areas for future research.

Abstract: Video reasoning models are a core component of egocentric and embodied agents. However, standard benchmarks for assessing models provide only evaluation of the output (e.g. the answer to a question), without evaluation of intermediate reasoning steps, and most provide answers only in the text domain. We introduce Minerva-Ego, a benchmark for evaluating complex egocentric visual reasoning. We extend recent high-quality video data sources recorded from egocentric / embodied settings with a set of challenging, multi-step multimodal questions and spatiotemporally-dense human-annotated reasoning traces. Benchmarking experiments show that state-of-the-art models still have a large gap to human performance. To investigate this gap in detail, we annotate each reasoning trace in the dataset with the objects of interest required to solve the question, as spatiotemporal mask annotations. Through extensive evaluations, we identify that prompting frontier models with hints of 'where' and 'when' to look yields substantial improvements in performance. Minerva-Ego can be downloaded at https://github.com/google-deepmind/neptune.

</details>


### [7] [Discretizing Group-Convolutional Neural Networks for 3D Geometry in Feature Space](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15368)
*Daniel Franzen,Jean Philip Filling,Michael Wand*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Feature-space sampling for GCNNs reduces computational cost by selecting representative transformed features based on similarity, decoupling geometric resolution from compute/memory, and enabling faster training of 3D equivariant classifiers with minimal accuracy loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Group-convolutional networks incur exponential growth in computations when sampling dense symmetry groups, especially in 3D (translations + rotations). There is a need to scale equivariant models to practical sizes without sacrificing accuracy.

Method: Replace geometry-based dense sampling with feature-space sampling: select a subset of transformed features that are representative according to feature similarity. This decouples geometric resolution from memory/compute, enables precomputation based on geometric similarity, and uses coarse feature-space sampling to preserve performance while accelerating training/inference for 3D equivariant classifiers.

Result: Empirically, coarse feature-space sampling preserves classification accuracy well and enables substantial acceleration of training via precomputation, reducing computational burden in 3D GCNNs while maintaining performance.

Conclusion: Feature-space sampling offers a viable, scalable trade-off between accuracy and efficiency in GCNNs, enabling efficient training and inference for equivariant 3D classifiers and potentially extending to other symmetry groups by leveraging feature-based representation similarity.

Abstract: Group-convolutional neural networks (GCNNs) are among the most important methods for introducing symmetry as an inductive bias in deep learning: In each linear layer, GCNNs sample a transformation group $G$ densely and correlate data and filters in different poses (with suitable anti-aliasing for steerable GCNNs) to maintain equivariance with respect to $G$. Unfortunately, applying filters to many data items resulting from this sampling is expensive (even for translations alone, i.e., in ordinary CNNs), and costs grow exponentially with increasing degrees of freedom (such as translations and rotations in 3D), which often hinders practical applications. In this paper, we propose sampling in feature space, i.e., replacing geometrically dense samples with representative samples selected by feature similarity. This decouples geometric resolution from memory and processing costs during training and inference, providing a novel way to trade off computational effort and accuracy. Our main empirical finding is that a coarse feature-space sampling already preserves classification accuracy remarkably well, which permits precomputation based on geometric similarity, accelerating the training of equivariant 3D classifiers substantially.

</details>


### [8] [ChangeFlow -- Latent Rectified Flow for Change Detection in Remote Sensing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15375)
*Blaž Rolih,Matic Fučka,Filip Wolf,Luka Čehovin Zajc*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Proposes ChangeFlow, a latent-space generative framework for RSCD that synthesizes change masks via rectified flow, enabling sampling-based predictions and improved global coherence, achieving 80.4% average F1 across four benchmarks, surpassing prior methods by 1.3 points, with speed comparable to discriminative baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Address limitations of per-pixel discriminative RSCD methods (lack of explicit region-level coherence) and existing generative methods (high computational cost, complex conditioning). Leverage latent-space synthesis and lightweight conditioning to model alternatives and enable sampling/uncertainty estimation.

Method: ChangeFlow reformulates change detection as latent-space mask synthesis using rectified flow; uses a structured, lightweight conditioning signal; stochastic design enables sampling-based prediction ensembling; aggregation improves robustness and sample agreement yields confidence estimates, enabling ambiguity highlighting.

Result: Average F1 80.4% on four benchmarks; +1.3 points vs previous best; inference speed similar to strong baselines.

Conclusion: Generative latent-space change mask synthesis with rectified flow offers robust, globally coherent predictions and practical uncertainty estimation for RSCD, narrowing the gap to discriminative methods while preserving efficiency.

Abstract: Remote sensing change detection (RSCD) aims to localise changes between two images of the same geographic region. In practice, change masks often follow region-level annotation conventions rather than purely local appearance differences, making them context-dependent and occasionally ambiguous. Most state-of-the-art methods utilise per-pixel discriminative classification, which produces a single prediction per input and fails to explicitly model the changed region as a coherent whole. A natural alternative is generative formulation, which can model a distribution of plausible masks, enabling sampling to capture ambiguity and encourage global consistency. However, existing generative RSCD approaches typically lag behind strong discriminative baselines due to the high computational cost of pixel-space generation and the complexity of their conditioning mechanisms. To address the limitations of prior discriminative and generative methods, we propose ChangeFlow, a generative framework that reformulates change detection as the synthesis of a change mask in latent space via rectified flow. ChangeFlow is guided by a structured yet lightweight conditioning signal, and its stochastic design naturally supports sampling-based prediction ensembling. Namely, aggregating multiple predicted change masks improves robustness, while sample agreement provides a practical confidence estimation that highlights ambiguous regions. Across four benchmarks, ChangeFlow achieves an average F1 of 80.4\%, improving by 1.3 points on average over the previous best method, while maintaining inference speed comparable to recent strong baselines. Project page: https://blaz-r.github.io/changeflow_cd

</details>


### [9] [MorphoHELM: A Comprehensive Benchmark for Evaluating Representations for Microscopy-Based Morphology Assays](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15383)
*Emre Hayir,Lorin Crawford,Alex X. Lu*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: MorphoHELM is a comprehensive open benchmark for evaluating Cell Painting feature extractors, assessing multiple methods across diverse tasks with varying batch effects to reveal degradation of biological signal and trade-offs; no single method dominates; classical computer vision approaches remain strong baselines; resources are public.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Evaluation in this domain is fragmented, with disparate tasks, datasets, pipelines, and metrics making fair cross-method comparison difficult. There is a need for a standardized, robust benchmark that probes robustness to technical noise and reveals trade-offs between methods.

Method: Develop a comprehensive benchmark that consolidates and extends evaluation standards for Cell Painting feature extraction. It evaluates the widest range of methods across multiple tasks, each under different degrees of batch effects (technical noise), quantifying how biological signal degrades as noise increases and enabling assessment of trade-offs between methods.

Result: MorphoHELM aggregates standardized evaluations, broadens robustness and coverage to the widest range of methods to date, and demonstrates that performance trade-offs exist across signal types. It finds no model outperforming classic computer vision strategies across all settings; CV-based approaches remain the strongest general-use representations.

Conclusion: The benchmark provides open datasets, code, and evaluation tools to enable fair, comprehensive comparison of feature extraction methods for Cell Painting, guiding method development toward robust, generalizable representations and facilitating broader adoption in morphological profiling.

Abstract: Microscopy images contain rich information about how cells respond to perturbations, making them essential to applications like drug screening. To quantify images, researchers often use representation extraction methods, and recent years have seen a proliferation of deep learning methods. While measuring the quality of these representations is essential, evaluation remains fragmented, with each proposed model evaluated on different tasks and datasets, using custom pipelines and metrics, making it difficult to fairly compare models. Here, we introduce MorphoHELM, a comprehensive open benchmark for evaluating feature extraction methods for Cell Painting, the most widely-used morphological profiling assay. MorphoHELM consolidates evaluation standards in the field, extends and corrects them to be more robust, and evaluates on the widest range of methods to date. A defining feature of the benchmark is that each task is evaluated at different degrees of batch effects (or technical noise), directly quantifying how the ability of methods to detect biological signal degrades as noise increases. Together, these properties enable MorphoHELM to detect trade-offs between methods, and we demonstrate that models that excel at certain kinds of biological signal are weaker at others. We show that no existing model outperforms classic computer vision analytic strategies across all settings, which remain the strongest general use-case representations. All datasets, code, and evaluation tools are publicly available at https://github.com/microsoft/MorphoHELM.

</details>


### [10] [PanoWorld: Geometry-Consistent Panoramic Video World Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15391)
*Le Jiang,Xiangyu Bai,Bishoy Galoaa,Shayda Moezzi,Caleb James Lee,Tooba Imtiaz,Edmund Yeh,Jennifer Dy,Yanzhi Wang,Sarah Ostadabbas*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: PanoWorld introduces a geometry- and dynamics-consistent panoramic video model that generates 360° video from a single image and caption, using depth and trajectory regularizers, plus spherical geometry conditioning, and introduces the PanoGeo dataset; it improves geometric consistency while preserving visual realism, with code released.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Panoramic video generation often focuses on visual realism but lacks explicit constraints on the underlying 3D scene state, leading to depth inconsistencies and poor temporal/spatial coherence on the sphere. A geometry- and dynamics-aware formulation is needed to support embodied AI tasks.

Method: Extend a pre-trained perspective video world model with two lightweight regularizers: (1) depth consistency loss against pseudo ground-truth panoramic depth, and (2) trajectory consistency loss for 3D world-frame positions of tracked points over time. Apply spherical-geometry-aware conditioning and positional encoding. Introduce PanoGeo, a unified geometry-aware panoramic video dataset for training and evaluation on real and synthetic data.

Result: Experiments show improved geometric consistency over prior panoramic generation methods while maintaining competitive visual realism.

Conclusion: Geometric modeling is essential for panoramic video generation to support holistic spatial understanding in embodied AI; PanoGeo provides a dataset for training/evaluation; code is publicly available.

Abstract: We present PanoWorld, a panoramic video world model that generates geometry-consistent 360$\degree$ video from a single image and a caption. Existing panoramic video methods optimize primarily for visual realism and do not explicitly constrain the underlying 3D scene state, producing outputs that appear plausible yet exhibit inconsistent depth, broken correspondences, and implausible motion across the spherical surface. We address this gap by framing panoramic video generation as a geometry- and dynamics-consistent latent state modeling problem rather than pure visual synthesis. Building on a pre-trained perspective video world model, we introduce two lightweight regularizers: a depth consistency loss against pseudo ground-truth panoramic depth, and a trajectory consistency loss that supervises the 3D world-frame positions of tracked points across time. We further apply spherical-geometry-aware adaptation to the conditioning and positional encoding. We additionally introduce PanoGeo, a unified geometry-aware panoramic video dataset with consistent depth, trajectory, and prompt annotations across diverse real and synthetic sources, used for both training and stratified evaluation. Experiments show that PanoWorld improves geometric consistency over prior panoramic generation methods while maintaining competitive visual realism, establishing that panoramic video generation must be treated as a geometric modeling problem to support the holistic spatial understanding requirements of embodied AI applications. Code is available at https://github.com/ostadabbas/PanoWorld.

</details>


### [11] [ELDOR: A Dataset and Benchmark for Illegal Gold Mining in the Amazon Rainforest](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15397)
*Kangning Cui,Surendra Bohara,Suraj Prasai,Zishan Shao,Wei Tang,Martin Pillaca,Edwin Flores,Zhen Yang,Gregory Larsen,Evan Dethier,David Lutz,Jean-Michel Morel,Miles Silman,Victor Pauca,Fan Yang*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: ELDOR is a large-scale UAV-based benchmark for illegal gold mining in the Amazon, featuring >2,500 ha with pixel-level labels for mining and ecological structures, four tasks (semantic segmentation, segmentation-derived recognition, direct multi-label classification, class-presence recognition with vision-language models), with cross-model evaluations under a closed-set protocol; underscores challenges in detecting rare small-scale mining features and fine-grained recovery classes; includes an interactive explorer for domain experts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Address fine-scale monitoring gaps caused by cloud cover and limited satellite resolution; provide a unified, richly labeled dataset and standardized benchmarks to advance remote-sensing and multimodal models for illegal mining detection and ecological impact assessment.

Method: Manual annotation of orthomosaic imagery covering 2,500+ hectares to produce pixel-level semantic labels for mining-related activities and surrounding ecological structures; define four benchmark tasks; perform controlled experiments comparing generic and RS-specific segmentation models, vision foundation model-based methods, direct multi-label classifiers, and vision–language models under a closed-set protocol; develop an interactive explorer for domain experts.

Result: Current methods struggle with rare small-scale mining structures and fine-grained recovery classes, indicating the need for context-aware and multimodal approaches; the benchmark dataset and tasks enable systematic evaluation across model families.

Conclusion: ELDOR provides a unified annotated data source and benchmarking suite for monitoring environmental disturbance from illegal mining in the rainforest, highlighting methodological gaps and enabling practical domain tools through an interactive explorer.

Abstract: Illegal gold mining in the Amazon rainforest causes deforestation, water contamination, and long-term ecosystem disruption, yet remains difficult to monitor at fine spatial scales. Satellite imagery supports large-scale observation, but often misses small mining-related structures and subtle land-cover transitions, especially under frequent cloud cover. We introduce ELDOR, a large-scale UAV benchmark for monitoring environmental and landscape disturbance from illegal gold mining in the rainforest. ELDOR contains manually annotated orthomosaic imagery covering over 2,500 hectares, with pixel-level semantic labels for both mining-related activities and surrounding ecological structures. With this unified annotation source, we establish four benchmark tasks: semantic segmentation, segmentation-derived recognition, direct multi-label classification, and class-presence recognition with vision-language models. Across these tasks, we compare generic and remote-sensing-specific segmentation models, vision foundation model-related segmentation methods, direct multi-label classification methods, and vision-language models under a controlled closed-set protocol. Results show that current methods still struggle with rare small-scale mining structures and fine-grained recovery classes, suggesting the need for context-aware and multimodal modeling. To support domain analysis and practical use, we further build an interactive explorer for domain experts that provides a unified interface for data exploration and model inference.

</details>


### [12] [U-SEG: Uncertainty in SEGmentation -- A systematic multi-variable exploration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15421)
*Michael Smith,Frank P. Ferrie*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Uncertainty estimation in segmentation is highly task- and data-dependent; panoptic tasks are more challenging; ensembles can help under right deployment conditions; time-series and sample diversity offer limited gains, mainly for calibration.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To systematically quantify how factors such as dataset difficulty, backbone, and downstream tasks affect segmentation uncertainty estimates, and to assess the benefits of time-series inputs, sample diversity, and ensembles.

Method: A large-scale framework evaluating semantic and panoptic segmentation across multiple datasets and backbones, comparing deterministic models, ensembles, time-series inputs, and diversity-based calibration approaches across downstream tasks.

Result: Panoptic segmentation is harder with greater cross-dataset/backbone performance variance; time-series samples help only in specific configurations and often are not cost-effective; sample diversity mainly aids calibration and generally does not beat simpler methods; ensembles offer notable gains when deployment conditions permit.

Conclusion: Guidance for practitioners: use deterministic models for tasks where they suffice; consider ensembles where deployment allows; leverage sample diversity to improve calibration; evaluate time-series inputs cautiously due to cost-benefit; generalization across datasets is not guaranteed.

Abstract: In this study, we explore in depth a few under-studied topics at the intersection of uncertainty estimation and segmentation. Prior work has shown that the quality of uncertainty estimates can be very sensitive to a range of variables. As one of the main uses of uncertainty estimation is to help identify and deal with prediction errors in practical scenarios, any factors that affect this must be clearly identified. For example, do more challenging domains or different datasets and architectures result in worse performance when using uncertainty estimates? Can prior frames in a video sequence in fact provide useful uncertainty estimates comparable to other approaches? Is it possible to combine uncertainty estimation approaches, taking advantage of sample diversity, to get better estimates? Finally, when might it make sense to use an ensemble-based uncertainty estimate over a deterministic network? We address these questions by creating a framework for and executing a large scale study across many variables such as datasets, backbones, and downstream tasks, for both semantic and panoptic segmentation. We find that a) the more challenging task of panoptic segmentation usually results in worse performance while high performance variance between datasets and backbones indicates that generalization is not guaranteed, b) time series samples can be useful for specific configurations, but in many cases are not worth the cost, c) sample diversity shows the most promise in the downstream task of calibration, but otherwise fails to beat simpler alternatives, d) a deterministic approach is adequate for some downstream tasks, but ensembles allow for significant improvements if the right conditions can be achieved in deployment.

</details>

Method: The approach combines (1) a multi-resolution inference schedule that toggles between full and low resolution frames to save compute, (2) ByteTrack-based cross-frame data association to link detections over time, and (3) a Rescore algorithm that aggregates detection confidences across frames using probability union rules to correct misclassifications.

We apply our approach to both a CNN-based detector and a Transformer-based model, demonstrating its generality across architectures with fundamentally different spatial processing. Experiments on ImageNetVID demonstrate that MR2-ByteTrack maintains accuracy, achieving mAP scores of up to 49.0 for the CNN-based models and 48.7 for the Transformer, while reducing multiply-accumulate operations by as much as 53\% for the CNNs and 32\% for the Transformer. When deployed on GAP9, an ultra-low-power RISC-V multicore MCU, our method yields up to 55\% energy savings compared to processing only full-resolution images, enabling the first real-time Transformer-based VOD on an MCU-class embedded vision node. <details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To overcome limitations of RGB-only and heterogeneous (frequency/spatially transformed) representations in COS by leveraging homogeneous illumination-reflectance decomposition, which preserves discriminability within the same spatial domain and exploits anti-correlations between illumination and reflectance across physical camouflage processes.

Method: Propose Retinex-based decomposition learned end-to-end (Task-Driven Retinex Decomposition); introduce a Discriminability Gap Attention module that leverages decomposition gains; apply a Camouflage-Breaking Contrastive loss in the reflectance space; support claims with a Discriminability Gap Theorem asserting anti-correlated illumination/reflectance across COS tasks and improved discriminability; not limited to a single dataset.

Abstract: Concealed Object Segmentation (COS) encompasses a family of dense-prediction tasks, including camouflaged object detection, polyp segmentation, transparent object detection, and industrial defect inspection, where targets are visually entangled with their surroundings through different physical mechanisms. Existing methods either operate directly on RGB images or employ \emph{heterogeneous} decompositions (\eg, Fourier, wavelet) that redistribute spatial evidence across scale/frequency coefficients, making pixel-aligned cues less direct. We introduce a fundamentally different perspective: \textbf{homogeneous image decomposition} via Retinex theory, which factorizes an image into illumination and reflectance components within the \emph{same} spatial domain. Our key insight is that visual entanglement enforces appearance matching in the composite space, but this does \emph{not} necessitate simultaneous matching in both component spaces, a phenomenon we formalize as the \textbf{Discriminability Gap Theorem}. Crucially, we show that across diverse COS sub-tasks, the underlying physical processes systematically anti-correlate illumination and reflectance differences, yielding theoretical guarantees that Retinex decomposition preserves or strictly improves total foreground--background discriminability across the full physical regime, with anti-correlation maximizing the gain. Building on this, we propose \textbf{RIDE} comprising: (i) a Task-Driven Retinex Decomposition module that learns segmentation-optimal factorizations end-to-end; (ii) a Discriminability Gap Attention mechanism that adaptively exploits where decomposition helps; and (iii) a Camouflage-Breaking Contrastive loss operating in reflectance feature space.

### [20] [When Does Sparse MoE Help in Vision? The Role of Backbone Compute Leverage in Sparse Routing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15484)
*Libo Sun,Po-wei Harn,Peixiong He,Xiao Qin*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Sparse top-k MoE with capacity constraints can improve vision accuracy when a substantial portion of FLOPs is routed and multiple experts are used, but gains are dataset- and scale-dependent. On ImageNet-scale, ρ alone is insufficient; multi-expert routing (k >= 2) is required. Per-sample routing suffers from batch-dispatch failure modes, while soft MoE over experts helps in some datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To assess the practical viability of sparse mixture-of-experts (MoE) for vision classification, focusing on compute-leverage (ρ) and the role of multi-expert routing under hard capacity constraints across standard benchmarks and seeds.

Method: Empirical study of sparse top-k routing with hard capacity constraints across CIFAR-10/100, Tiny-ImageNet, ImageNet-1K with multi-seed protocols. Two controlled experiments: (1) a hidden-size sweep on CIFAR-10 to test backbone generality; (2) an ImageNet-1K ablation varying only top-k while keeping architecture, initialization, and ρ fixed. Additionally, a per-sample Soft MoE variant that softmaxes over experts (vs. batch dispatch) is evaluated on CIFAR-100.

Result: Key findings include a compute-leverage pattern: positive accuracy gaps require a substantial routed FLOPs fraction ρ; at ImageNet scale, ρ is necessary but not sufficient, and multi-expert routing (k≥2) is required. The CIFAR-10 hidden-size sweep shows sign reversals across backbones, suggesting backbone family is not the active variable. The ImageNet-1K ablation flipping top-k only (with ρ fixed) flips the gap from positive to negative across all seeds. A per-sample Soft MoE variant that centers the dispatch on the batch axis rescues CIFAR-100 above dense baselines, implicating batch-axis dispatch as the dominant failure mode in per-sample CNN settings.

Conclusion: Sparse MoE in vision faces nontrivial trade-offs: substantial routing (high ρ) and multiple experts are essential for gains, particularly at large scale; backbone or architecture alone does not resolve the issue, and per-sample routing suffers from batch-dispatch problems that softening the MoE over the batch can mitigate. Overall, the results emphasize careful design choices (k, ρ, routing axis, and capacity) to realize efficiency and accuracy benefits; code and results are available at the provided link.

Abstract: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) networks promise favorable accuracy-compute trade-offs, yet practical vision deployments are hindered by expert collapse and limited end-to-end efficiency gains. We study when sparse top-$k$ routing with hard capacity constraints helps in vision classification, evaluated under multi-seed protocols on four benchmarks (CIFAR-10/100, Tiny-ImageNet, ImageNet-1K). We observe a \emph{compute-leverage pattern}: positive accuracy gaps require a substantial fraction $ρ$ of total FLOPs to be routed; at ImageNet scale this is necessary but not sufficient, as multi-expert routing ($k \geq 2$) is additionally required. Two controlled experiments isolate these factors. A hidden-size sweep on CIFAR-10 yields both predicted sign reversals across standard and depthwise backbones, ruling out backbone family as the active variable. An ImageNet-1K ablation that varies only top-$k$ -- holding architecture, initialization, and $ρ$ fixed -- reverses the gap from positive to negative across all five seeds. A per-sample variant of Soft MoE that softmaxes over experts rather than the batch rescues CIFAR-100 above the dense baseline, identifying batch-axis dispatch as the dominant failure mode in per-sample CNN settings. Code and aggregate results: https://github.com/libophd/sparse-moe-vision-rho.

Method: Structural Noise Initialization Strategy (SNIS) assigns higher noise to edited regions and lower noise to unedited regions to create a favorable starting point; Noise Guidance Mechanism (NGM) uses the video prior in the generative model to integrate rich latent information and guide denoising, preserving content and coherence in a tuning-free, instruction-based framework.

Abstract: Video editing poses a significant challenge. While a series of tuning-free methods circumvent the need for extensive data collection and model training, they often underutilize the rich information embedded within noisy latent, leading to unsatisfactory results. To address this, we propose a \textit{tuning-free, instruction-based} video editing framework. We approach video editing from the perspective of noisy latent: we design a Structural Noise Initialization Strategy (SNIS) to secure a superior editing starting point by assigning higher noise levels to edited regions (to facilitate content change) and lower noise levels to unedited regions (to maintain content consistency). We introduce a Noise Guidance Mechanism (NGM), which leverages the video prior in the generative model and effectively integrates rich information within the noisy latent to guide the denoising process, thereby preserving unedited content and overall visual coherence. ### [34] [Neutral-Reference Prompting for Vision-Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15615)
*Senmao Tian,Xiang Wei,Shunli Zhang*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: NeRP is a plug‑and‑play prompting-correction method for vision–language models that improves unseen-class recognition without hurting base-class accuracy by using neutral prompts and reference images to measure class priors and then flipping predictions between confusable pairs when priors are misleading.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address the Base-New Trade-off in VLMs, where improving performance on unseen classes often degrades base-class accuracy, and to counteract asymmetric, prior-driven mispredictions that persist after pretraining.

Method: Compute class-wise priors from neutral prompts and reference images along the pre-trained inter-class geometry. Combine priors with the sample likelihood to form a surrogate score. If priors strongly favor an incorrect prediction while evidence is weak, perform a local flip between easily confusable class pairs (no model parameter updates). Implemented as a plug‑in prompting correction strategy (NeRP).

Result: Across multiple backbones and 15 few-shot and cross-domain benchmarks, NeRP substantially improves unseen-class accuracy while preserving base-class predictions.

Conclusion: NeRP provides a lightweight, effective mechanism to mitigate BNT in VLMs by leveraging prompting geometry and evidence-driven corrections, with strong empirical support across diverse tasks; potential extensions include application to other modalities and prompting schemes.

Abstract: Efficient transfer learning of vision-language models (VLMs) commonly suffers from a Base-New Trade-off (BNT): improving performance on unseen (new) classes often degrades accuracy on known (base) classes. Addressing how to boost recognition of unseen classes without sacrificing known-class performance remains a central challenge. Existing work often simplistically attributes the BNT to overfitting on known classes. We observe an interesting phenomenon: VLMs frequently exhibit asymmetric confusion on certain downstream data, i.e., samples of class A are systematically mispredicted as class B, while the reverse confusion (B to A) rarely occurs. For known classes, this kind of bias can be mitigated by tuning using a cross-entropy loss, but for unseen classes, such pretraining-induced bias persists and harms generalization. Motivated by this, we propose NeRP, a plug-and-play prompting correction strategy that improves discrimination on unseen classes without modifying model parameters. NeRP leverages neutral text prompts and reference images to measure class-wise prior preferences along the pre-trained inter-class geometry, and combines them with the sample likelihood to obtain the model's surrogate score. If, for a given sample, the prior strongly favors the current prediction while the observed evidence is clearly insufficient, we perform a local flip between easily confusable class pairs, thereby correcting prior-dominated mispredictions. Extensive experiments across multiple backbones and 15 few-shot and cross-domain benchmarks show that NeRP substantially improves accuracy on unseen classes while preserving known-class prediction performance.

</details>

### [36] [LRCP: Low-Rank Compressibility Guided Visual Token Pruning for Efficient LVLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15621)
*Hongyu Lu,Feng Zhang,Wenwei Jin,Huanling Hu,Tianjun Shi,Shikai Jiang,Yao Hu,Jiawei Li*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: LRCP is a training-free visual token compression method that uses PCA to identify a dominant low-rank subspace of visual tokens and retains tokens poorly explained by this subspace, enabling high token reduction with minimal performance loss in image/video understanding.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LVLMs suffer from rising inference costs as visual tokens increase; attention-based scoring can introduce positional bias; representation-based methods may miss global structure. The authors observe a stable, dominant low-rank subspace in token representations across models/datasets and seek a global, training-free compression strategy.

Method: Estimate the dominant low-rank subspace of visual tokens via PCA on token representations. For each token, compute its projection residual onto this subspace and retain tokens with large residuals (poorly explained by the low-rank background). This procedure is training-free and applied to images and videos across models/datasets.

Result: LRCP preserves 94.7% of image-understanding performance with an 88.9% token reduction and 97.8% of video-understanding accuracy with an 87.5% token reduction, demonstrating strong token-efficiency gains.

Conclusion: LRCP is an effective, training-free framework leveraging the observed low-rank structure of visual tokens to achieve substantial token compression with minimal performance loss, generalizing across image and video tasks. Potential limitations include linearity of PCA and reliance on a stable dominant subspace; future work could explore nonlinear extensions or adaptive subspace tracking.

Abstract: Large vision-language models (LVLMs) achieve strong multimodal understanding, but their inference cost grows rapidly with the number of visual tokens, especially for high-resolution images and long videos. Existing attention-based methods estimate token importance from attention scores, which may introduce positional bias, while representation-based methods reduce visual redundancy based on feature relations or reconstruction errors, overlooking the global structure of the visual token set. In this paper, we revisit visual token compression from the perspective of low-rank compressibility. Across models and datasets, we observe that visual token representations exhibit a pronounced low-rank structure, with a dominant subspace that remains stable even after a large fraction of tokens is randomly removed. Motivated by this finding, we propose LRCP, a training-free compression framework that first estimates the dominant low-rank subspace of visual tokens via PCA, and then scores each token by its projection residual onto this subspace, retaining tokens that are poorly explained by the low-rank background. Extensive experiments show that LRCP achieves superior results, preserving 94.7% of the original image-understanding performance with an 88.9% token reduction and 97.8% of the average video-understanding accuracy with an 87.5% token reduction.

</details>

### [39] [VAGS: Velocity Adaptive Guidance Scale for Image Editing and Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15661)
*Yan Luo,Ahmadou Aidara,Jingyi Lu,Jeremy Moebel,Kai Han,Mengyu Wang*

Main category: cs.CV

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard classifier-free guidance uses a fixed scale across the entire ODE trajectory, which mismatches the evolving signal strength and dynamics; the guidance value should depend on local compatibility between guidance velocity and model dynamics.

Method: Define a bounded multiplicative factor that combines a temporal signal level with the cosine similarity between relevant velocity fields. Two variants: VAGS for inversion-free editing (alignment between source- and target-guided velocities) and VAGS-Gen for generation (alignment between unconditional and conditional velocities). No fine-tuning, no auxiliary networks, no extra forward passes; fixed CFG is recovered as a special case.

Result: Empirical evaluation on PIE-Bench and DIV2K for editing, and COCO17, CUB-200, Flickr30K for generation shows consistent improvements in structural fidelity and generation quality over fixed CFG and other training-free guidance baselines.

Conclusion: VAGS provides a training-free, adaptive guidance mechanism that improves both editing and generation tasks by adjusting guidance strength to local velocity alignment; code available at the authors' GitHub.

Abstract: Classifier-free guidance (CFG) is the primary control over how strongly text semantics move a flow-based sampler, yet standard practice holds its scale fixed across the entire ODE trajectory. This is a fundamental mismatch: early steps are noise-dominated and carry weak semantic signal, while late steps commit image structure and demand stronger directional commitment; more critically, the value of any guidance strength depends on whether the guided velocity is consistent with the model's current dynamics or working against them. We propose \textit{Velocity-Adaptive Guidance Scale} (VAGS), a training-free replacement that multiplies the nominal scale by a bounded factor combining a temporal signal-level term with the cosine similarity between task-relevant velocity fields. For inversion-free editing, VAGS measures the alignment between source- and target-guided velocities, so edit strength at each step reflects local compatibility between preservation and transformation. For generation, VAGS-Gen uses the alignment between unconditional and conditional velocities as the analogous signal. Neither variant requires fine-tuning, auxiliary networks, or extra forward passes, and fixed CFG is recovered as a special case. On PIE-Bench and DIV2K for editing, and COCO17, CUB-200, and Flickr30K for generation, VAGS consistently improves structural fidelity and generation quality over fixed CFG and recent training-free guidance variants. The code is publicly available at https://github.com/Harvard-AI-and-Robotics-Lab/Velocity_Adaptive_Guidance_Scale.

To isolate this ability, we design controlled tracing tasks that introduce nearby competitors while reducing semantic and topological ambiguity such as crossings and overlaps. Across these tasks, even state-of-the-art VLMs frequently lose the target path and switch to nearby alternatives, especially when those alternatives look locally similar to the target. Behavioral interventions and internal analyses indicate that these failures arise from local competition: nearby similar distractors pull the model away from the true continuation. Standard remedies do not remove this bottleneck: model-size scaling provides only limited gains, reasoning partially compensates through costly substitute strategies, and explicit tracing instructions fail to recover stable path following. Finally, tests on tangled-cable scenes and metro maps with richer visual complexity show that the same path-switching failure persists beyond our controlled settings.

### [47] [EntropyScan: Towards Model-level Backdoor Detection in LVLMs via Visual Attention Entropy](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15711)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Extracts visual attention distributions from the initial layers of the LLM, computes Tsallis entropy to quantify structural distortions in cross-modal alignment, and uses a reference-anchored Z-score normalization on a small benign sample set to identify backdoored models. The approach is trigger-agnostic and lightweight.

However, identifying whether a given model is backdoored remains a critical but unexplored task. To fill this gap, we propose EntropyScan, a lightweight and trigger-agnostic method for model-level backdoor detection in LVLMs. We first observe that backdoor injection disrupts the cross-modal alignment, resulting in pronounced structural anomalies in visual attention allocation on benign samples. Based on this insight, EntropyScan detects the backdoor models by quantifying such attention deviations. Specifically, it extracts visual attention distributions from the initial layers of the Large Language Model (LLM) and applies Tsallis entropy to capture these structural distortions. By employing a reference-anchored Z-score normalization on a small set of benign samples, it effectively identifies the backdoored model. Extensive experiments across two LVLMs architectures and three advanced attack scenarios show that EntropyScan achieves an F1 score of 98.5% in average and an AUC of 96.6%. Our code will be publicly available soon.

### [48] [STABLE: Simulation-Ready Tabletop Layout Generation via a Semantics-Physics Dual System](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16137)
*Zhen Luo,Yixuan Yang,Xudong Xu,Jinkun Hao,Zhaoyang Lyu,Feng Zheng,Jiangmiao Pang,Yanwei Fu*

TL;DR: STABLE introduces a semantics-physics dual-system for simulation-ready tabletop scene generation from task instructions, combining a fine-tuned Semantic Reasoner with a physics-aware Physics Corrector to iteratively refine layouts into physically plausible, instruction-faithful scenes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs alone struggle with 3D spatial reasoning, causing object collisions or floating in generated scenes. There is a need to jointly enforce semantic alignment to task goals and physical plausibility for simulation-ready environments.

Method: A two-module pipeline with progressive generation: (1) Semantic Reasoner: a fine-tuned LLM trained on a structured tabletop scene dataset to produce coarse layouts from task instructions; (2) Physics Corrector: a physics-aware flow-based denoising model that outputs pose updates to refine layouts. The system alternates between modules, expanding scenes from task-critical objects to background objects, to ensure both semantic fidelity and physical validity.

Result: STABLE generates simulation-ready tabletop scenes that strictly follow task instructions and exhibit significantly improved physical validity compared with prior approaches.

Conclusion: Decoupling semantic planning from physics refinement in a progressive, iterative loop yields robust, instruction-faithful and physically plausible tabletop scene generation, with potential applicability to broader embodied AI scene generation tasks.

Abstract: Generating simulation-ready tabletop scenes from task instructions is an intriguing and promising research direction in the field of Embodied AI. However, existing task-to-scene generation methods rely exclusively on large language models (LLMs) to predict scene layouts, inevitably yielding object collisions or floating due to LLMs' inherent limitations in 3D spatial reasoning. In this paper, we present STABLE, a semantics-physics dual-system tailored for simulation-ready tabletop scene generation. STABLE consists of two complementary modules: (i) a Semantic Reasoner, a fine-tuned LLM trained on a structured tabletop scene dataset to generate coarse layouts from input task instructions, and (ii) a Physics Corrector, a physics-aware flow-based denoising model that outputs pose updates to refine layouts, which ensures the physical plausibility of scenes while preserves semantic alignment with task instructions. STABLE adopts a progressive generation paradigm: by alternating between the Semantic Reasoner and Physics Corrector, it incrementally expands the scene from task-critical objects to background objects. ### [51] [DecomPose: Disentangling Cross-Category Optimization Contention for Category-Level 6D Object Pose Estimation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15728)
*Yifan Gao,Lu Zou,Zhangjin Huang,Guoping Wang*

TL;DR: DecomPose introduces a difficulty-aware decomposition to mitigate cross-category gradient contention in category-level 6D pose estimation by gradient decoupling and asymmetric branching, improving accuracy across benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Shared, fully parameterized networks for heterogeneous object categories induce conflicting optimization signals and negative transfer due to geometric heterogeneity. A diagnostic is needed to quantify module-level cross-category contention and guide a remedy.

Method: 1) Use gradient-based diagnostics to measure cross-category contention. 2) DecomPose: a two-pronged framework: (a) difficulty-aware gradient decoupling that groups categories by a data-driven difficulty proxy and routes each instance to a group-specific correspondence branch; (b) stability-driven asymmetric branching that assigns higher-capacity branches to simpler categories and lightweight branches to complex categories to stabilize training and reduce noisy updates.

Result: Empirical results on REAL275, CAMERA25, and HouseCat6D show reduced cross-category optimization contention and improved pose estimation performance across multiple benchmarks.

Conclusion: A difficulty-aware decomposition framework effectively mitigates cross-category gradient contention in multi-category pose estimation, delivering superior performance and greater training stability.

Abstract: Category-level 6D object pose estimation is typically formulated as a multi-category joint learning problem with fully shared model parameters. However, pronounced geometric heterogeneity across categories entangles incompatible optimization signals in shared modules, resulting in gradient conflicts and negative transfer during training. To address this challenge, we first introduce gradient-based diagnostics to quantify module-level cross-category contention. Building on results of diagnostics, we propose DecomPose, a difficulty-aware decomposition framework that mitigates optimization contention via: (1) difficulty-aware gradient decoupling, which groups categories using a data-driven difficulty proxy and routes each instance to a group-specific correspondence branch to isolate incompatible updates; and (2) stability-driven asymmetric branching, which assigns higher-capacity branches to structurally simple categories as stable optimization anchors while constraining complex categories with lightweight branches to suppress noisy updates and alleviate negative transfer. Extensive experiments on REAL275, CAMERA25, and HouseCat6D demonstrate that DecomPose effectively reduces cross-category optimization contention and delivers superior pose estimation performance across multiple benchmarks.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need to prevent catastrophic forgetting of semantic grounding while achieving robust control in manipulation tasks, including out-of-distribution generalization.

Method: Introduce a two-stream architecture by adding a parallel Dorsal Expert (analogous to the dorsal visuomotor pathway) to the VLA. Initialize the Dorsal Expert from a pretrained generative model and train it with a mid-level reasoning objective that predicts visual dynamics. Train the entire VLA end-to-end on action data with no parameter freezing or gradient stopping and without auxiliary VL co-training.

Result: The UAM preserves over 95% of the VLM’s multimodal capability and achieves the highest average success rate among baselines on manipulation tasks testing out-of-distribution generalization (unseen objects, novel object–target compositions, instruction variation).

Abstract: Vision--language--action (VLA) models are typically built by fine-tuning a pretrained vision--language model (VLM) on action data. However, we show that this standard recipe systematically erodes the VLM's multimodal competence, a side effect we call the embodiment tax. But do VLAs have to forget? Inspired by the two-stream organization of biological vision, we trace this degradation to a structural bottleneck: current VLAs ask a single encoder to support both language-grounded semantics and control-relevant visual features, whereas biological vision separates recognition and visuomotor control into distinct pathways. Building on this view, we propose the Unified Action Model (UAM), which adds a parallel Dorsal Expert, an analog of the brain's dorsal pathway. To make the Dorsal Expert an effective second pathway and reduce the control-learning burden on the VLM, we initialize it from a pretrained generative model and train it with a mid-level reasoning objective that predicts visual dynamics. This design allows us to train the whole VLA end-to-end on action data alone: with no parameter freezing, no gradient stopping, and no auxiliary VL co-training, UAM retains over $95\%$ of the underlying VLM's multimodal capability and at the same time achieves the highest average success rate among baselines on a variety of manipulation tasks that probe out-of-distribution generalization, including unseen objects, novel object--target compositions, and instruction variation. Together, these results suggest that semantic preservation in VLAs can emerge from architectural separation itself, rather than being enforced by frozen weights or auxiliary data replay, and that this preserved semantic capability can naturally transfer from VLMs to semantic generalization in actions.

### [54] [BARRIER: Bounded Activation Regions for Robust Information Erasure](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15737)
*Jan Miksa,Patryk Krukowski,Przemysław Spurek,Dawid Damian Rymarczyk,Marcin Sendera*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: BARRIER uses activation-space geometry with interval arithmetic on SVD-projected features to perform targeted, provably bounded unlearning, avoiding collateral forgetting and enabling strong erasure updates.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional weight-space unlearning often degrades neutral concepts due to lack of formal guarantees; there is a need for principled, provable protection of retained knowledge while erasing targeted concepts.

Method: Project activations via SVD, define a forget region as a bounding hypercube in this space, and apply Interval Arithmetic to constrain updates to the forget interval. The complement is probabilistically bounded to prevent unintended drift, enabling aggressive updates within the forget region.

Result: Empirically, BARRIER matches or surpasses state-of-the-art trade-offs on classifiers and diffusion models, achieving maximal erasure of targeted concepts with protection of all other representations.

Conclusion: BARRIER reframes unlearning as a geometry-based, formally-quantified optimization problem, providing rigorous guarantees and enabling aggressive, targeted erasure without collateral damage; code is available online.

Abstract: Machine unlearning has reached a critical bottleneck. As traditional weight-space interventions focus primarily on erasing targeted concepts, they often fail to prevent the unintended suppression of other significant representations. This leads to substantial collateral damage, with essential knowledge being forgotten, because these methods lack formal mathematical guarantees for the preservation of neutral concepts. To avoid degradation, they are frequently forced into conservative updates. We propose BARRIER (Bounded Activation Regions for Robust Information Erasure), a paradigm-shifting framework that shifts the locus of intervention from static model weights to the dynamic geometry of hidden-layer activations. Unlike existing methods, BARRIER employs Interval Arithmetic (IA) on SVD-based projections of the activation space to encapsulate the specific target region within a bounding hypercube. By driving unlearning updates exclusively within this forget interval and mathematically bounding the model response on the complement, we ensure rigorous protection of the retain distribution. This geometric construction transforms the preservation of knowledge from an empirical heuristic into a formal optimization target with a probabilistic tail bound on functional drift. Crucially, this stability permits highly aggressive unlearning updates within the forget region. Empirical evaluations demonstrate that BARRIER matches state-of-the-art trade-offs across classifiers and diffusion models, maximizing targeted concept erasure while safeguarding the integrity of all other representations. Our code is available at https://github.com/OneAndZero24/BARRIER.

</details>

To address this issue, we propose HyperDiT, a unified framework establishing Hyper-Connected Cross-Scale Interactions to bridge the semantic and pixel manifold. Diverging from injecting semantics by AdaLN, HyperDiT utilizes Cross-Attention mechanisms, enabling fine-grained tokens to query multi-level semantic anchors globally. To resolve the spatial mismatch during multi-scale interactions, we introduce Scale-Aware Rotary Position Embedding (SA-RoPE) to ensure precise geometric alignment among tokens of varying patch sizes. Method: Four components: (1) nonparametric 3D to rolled-equivalent 2D unwrapping without a global finger-shape model; (2) point-cloud fusion to register and mosaic multiple partial 3D captures; (3) ellipse-based pose normalization for canonical finger alignment; (4) pose-aware cross-modal registration to improve compatibility between 3D fingerprints and both contactless and contact-based 2D fingerprints.

Result: On a self-collected database of 150 fingers: 3D fusion error ~0.09 mm; contactless 2D–3D registration achieves ridge-scale projection accuracy; pose-aware unwrapping improves genuine matching scores versus generic 3D unwrapping; robust pose estimation and consistent gains in 2D compatibility.

Conclusion: 3D fingerprints can serve as an effective geometric bridge across heterogeneous fingerprint modalities; the proposed framework enables reliable preprocessing and registration to facilitate cross-modal matching between 3D, contactless 2D, and contact-based 2D fingerprints.

Abstract: Three-dimensional (3D) fingerprints preserve global finger geometry and local ridge structure while avoiding contact-induced deformation, but they remain difficult to integrate with legacy two-dimensional (2D) fingerprint systems. This paper addresses the intermediate stage between 3D acquisition and cross-modal matching, and presents a unified framework for 3D fingerprint preprocessing and registration across contactless and contact-based 2D modalities. The framework combines four components: 1) a nonparametric visualization and unwrapping method that converts a 3D fingerprint point cloud into a rolled-equivalent 2D representation without relying on a global finger-shape model; 2) a point-cloud fusion pipeline that registers and mosaics multiple partial 3D captures into a more complete fingerprint model; 3) an ellipse-based pose normalization method for canonical finger alignment; and 4) a pose-aware cross-modal registration strategy that improves compatibility between 3D fingerprints and both contactless and contact-based 2D fingerprints. Experiments on a self-collected multimodal fingerprint database containing 150 fingers show that the proposed framework achieves ridge-level 3D registration accuracy, robust pose estimation, and consistent gains in 2D compatibility. In particular, the 3D fusion error is concentrated around 0.09 mm, contactless 2D--3D registration reaches ridge-scale projection accuracy, and pose-aware unwrapping improves genuine matching scores relative to generic 3D unwrapping. These results support the use of 3D fingerprints as an effective geometric bridge across heterogeneous fingerprint modalities.

### [61] [Embedding-perturbed Exploration Preference Optimization for Flow Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15803)
*Sujie Hu,Chubin Chen,Jiashu Zhu,Jiahong Wu,Xiangxiang Chu,Xiu Li*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Proposes Embedding-perturbed Exploration Preference Optimization (E^2PO) to maintain intra-group variance via embedding-level perturbations, addressing instability and premature stagnation in group-based RL alignment (GRPO).

Existing remedies (noise, larger groups) are inadequate for preserving discriminative gradient signals during optimization.

Method: Introduce embedding-level perturbations within sample groups to sustain a robust, discriminative variance. The E^2PO framework injects structured perturbations at the embedding level, ensuring continued signal for policy optimization throughout training.

Result: Extensive experiments show that E^2PO outperforms state-of-the-art baselines, yielding more faithful alignment with human preferences.

Conclusion: Embedding-level perturbations provide a robust mechanism to maintain optimization signal in group-based RL alignment, improving stability and the quality of alignment with human judgments.

Abstract: Recent advancements have established Reinforcement Learning (RL) as a pivotal paradigm for aligning generative models with human intent. However, group-based optimization frameworks (e.g., GRPO) face a critical limitation: the rapid decay of intra-group variance. As the distinctiveness among samples within a group diminishes, the variance approaches zero. This eliminates the very learning signal required for optimization, rendering the process unstable and forcing the policy into premature stagnation or reward hacking. Existing strategies, such as varying the initial noise or increasing group sizes, often fail to address this fundamental issue, resulting in training instability or diminishing returns. To overcome these challenges, we propose $\textbf{Embedding-perturbed Exploration Preference Optimization (}E^2\textbf{PO)}$, a novel framework that sustains optimization through embedding-level perturbation. Our method introduces structured, embedding-level perturbations within sample groups, guaranteeing a robust variance that preserves the discriminative signal throughout the training process. Extensive experiments demonstrate that our approach significantly outperforms state-of-the-art baselines, achieving a more faithful alignment with human preference.
